C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 1 of 251

--- PAGE 2 ---
File I/O
Q1. Consider the stream state flags. If a file stream reaches the exact end-of-file during a read operation
loop 1, which state bits are set to true?
// Assume fin has read the last byte
if (fin.eof()) cout << "EOF";
if (fin.fail()) cout << "FAIL";
A. Both eofbit and failbit are set
B. Only eofbit is set
C. Only failbit is set
D. Both eofbit and badbit are set
Correct Answer: Both eofbit and failbit are set
Rationale: When a sequential read operation attempts to read past the end of a file, both the eofbit (signaling end of file)
and the failbit (signaling that the requested extraction failed) are set simultaneously. 
Q2. Analyze the following binary I/O structural trap where an object with a virtual method table is written
via stream.write() or read via stream.read():
struct PolymorphicContainer {
    virtual void render() {}
    int dataID = {i};
};
// ... out.write(reinterpret_cast(&obj), sizeof(obj));
A. It writes the raw internal vptr address to disk, causing critical undefined behavior or segmentation faults upon
reload
B. It safely serializes the virtual functions along with data fields
C. The compiler issues a static_assert failure because the class is not trivially copyable
D. The stream automatically excludes the vptr pointer during the write sequence
Correct Answer: It writes the raw internal vptr address to disk, causing critical undefined behavior or segmentation faults
upon reload
Rationale: Using raw byte-wise write operations on polymorphic objects copies the internal virtual table pointer (vptr).
When reloaded into a different memory space or session, this stale address points to invalid memory, corrupting the
execution context. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 2 of 251

--- PAGE 3 ---
Q3. What happens if you open a file using ios::out | ios::app combined with an explicit seekp() invocation
to position 30?
ofstream out("log_{i}.txt", ios::out | ios::app);
out.seekp(0, ios::beg);
out << "Data";
A. The output data is unconditionally appended to the end of the file, completely ignoring seekp() instructions
B. The data is overwritten at the beginning of the file due to seekp() override
C. The stream enters an internal fail state and rejects the output operation
D. The file contents are cleared before writing
Correct Answer: The output data is unconditionally appended to the end of the file, completely ignoring seekp()
instructions
Rationale: When a file stream is opened in append mode (ios::app), all output operations are forced to the current end-of-
file before every write, effectively rendering any seekp() relocations inert. 
Q4. What is the exact functional distinction between using the method close() explicitly vs relying on the
stream class destructor instance 4?
{
    ofstream logFile("trace_{i}.txt");
    logFile << "Payload";
    // Line X: explicit close or let it go out of scope
}
A. Both flush buffers and release handles, but calling close() allows early resource reuse and explicit error
verification via fail()
B. The destructor does not automatically flush data buffers, causing potential silent data loss
C. Calling close() manually causes a double-free runtime panic when the destructor runs out of scope
D. The destructor can propagate file write errors through exception chains, whereas close() cannot
Correct Answer: Both flush buffers and release handles, but calling close() allows early resource reuse and explicit error
verification via fail()
Rationale: The destructor automatically closes the file handle and flushes remaining buffers, but it cannot safely return
errors or allow the program to verify successful closure before exiting the block scope. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 3 of 251

--- PAGE 4 ---
Q5. What is the behavior of the following C++ code snippet when opening a non-existent file for reading
with ifstream?
#include 
#include 
using namespace std;
int main() {
    ifstream file("ghost_file_{i}.txt");
    if (file) cout << "Success";
    else cout << "Failure";
    return 0;
}
A. Prints 'Failure' safely
B. Throws a runtime_error exception
C. Prints 'Success' with undefined behavior
D. Causes a compile-time syntax error
Correct Answer: Prints 'Failure' safely
Rationale: By default, ifstream does not throw exceptions upon failure to open a file unless explicit exception masks are
set via file.exceptions(). Instead, the stream state bits are set, and evaluating the stream object as a boolean returns false.

Rationale: By default, ifstream does not throw exceptions upon failure to open a file unless explicit exception masks are
set via file.exceptions(). Instead, the stream state bits are set, and evaluating the stream object as a boolean returns false. 
Q36. Consider the stream state flags. If a file stream reaches the exact end-of-file during a read operation
loop 36, which state bits are set to true?
// Assume fin has read the last byte
if (fin.eof()) cout << "EOF";
if (fin.fail()) cout << "FAIL";
A. Both eofbit and failbit are set
B. Only eofbit is set
C. Only failbit is set
D. Both eofbit and badbit are set
Correct Answer: Both eofbit and failbit are set
Rationale: When a sequential read operation attempts to read past the end of a file, both the eofbit (signaling end of file)
and the failbit (signaling that the requested extraction failed) are set simultaneously. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 19 of 251

--- PAGE 20 ---
Q37. Analyze the following binary I/O structural trap where an object with a virtual method table is written
via stream.write() or read via stream.read():
struct PolymorphicContainer {
    virtual void render() {}
    int dataID = {i};
};
// ... out.write(reinterpret_cast(&obj), sizeof(obj));
A. It writes the raw internal vptr address to disk, causing critical undefined behavior or segmentation faults upon
reload
B. It safely serializes the virtual functions along with data fields
C. The compiler issues a static_assert failure because the class is not trivially copyable
D. The stream automatically excludes the vptr pointer during the write sequence
Correct Answer: It writes the raw internal vptr address to disk, causing critical undefined behavior or segmentation faults
upon reload
Rationale: Using raw byte-wise write operations on polymorphic objects copies the internal virtual table pointer (vptr).
When reloaded into a different memory space or session, this stale address points to invalid memory, corrupting the
execution context. 
Q38. What happens if you open a file using ios::out | ios::app combined with an explicit seekp() invocation
to position 380?
ofstream out("log_{i}.txt", ios::out | ios::app);
out.seekp(0, ios::beg);
out << "Data";
A. The output data is unconditionally appended to the end of the file, completely ignoring seekp() instructions
B. The data is overwritten at the beginning of the file due to seekp() override
C. The stream enters an internal fail state and rejects the output operation
D. The file contents are cleared before writing
Correct Answer: The output data is unconditionally appended to the end of the file, completely ignoring seekp()
instructions
Rationale: When a file stream is opened in append mode (ios::app), all output operations are forced to the current end-of-
file before every write, effectively rendering any seekp() relocations inert. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 20 of 251

--- PAGE 21 ---
Q39. What is the exact functional distinction between using the method close() explicitly vs relying on the
stream class destructor instance 39?
{
    ofstream logFile("trace_{i}.txt");
    logFile << "Payload";
    // Line X: explicit close or let it go out of scope
}
A. Both flush buffers and release handles, but calling close() allows early resource reuse and explicit error
verification via fail()
B. The destructor does not automatically flush data buffers, causing potential silent data loss
C. Calling close() manually causes a double-free runtime panic when the destructor runs out of scope
D. The destructor can propagate file write errors through exception chains, whereas close() cannot
Correct Answer: Both flush buffers and release handles, but calling close() allows early resource reuse and explicit error
verification via fail()
Rationale: The destructor automatically closes the file handle and flushes remaining buffers, but it cannot safely return
errors or allow the program to verify successful closure before exiting the block scope.

--- PAGE 37 ---
Q71. Consider the stream state flags. If a file stream reaches the exact end-of-file during a read operation
loop 71, which state bits are set to true?
// Assume fin has read the last byte
if (fin.eof()) cout << "EOF";
if (fin.fail()) cout << "FAIL";
A. Both eofbit and failbit are set
B. Only eofbit is set
C. Only failbit is set
D. Both eofbit and badbit are set
Correct Answer: Both eofbit and failbit are set
Rationale: When a sequential read operation attempts to read past the end of a file, both the eofbit (signaling end of file)
and the failbit (signaling that the requested extraction failed) are set simultaneously. 
Q72. Analyze the following binary I/O structural trap where an object with a virtual method table is written
via stream.write() or read via stream.read():
struct PolymorphicContainer {
    virtual void render() {}
    int dataID = {i};
};
// ... out.write(reinterpret_cast(&obj), sizeof(obj));
A. It writes the raw internal vptr address to disk, causing critical undefined behavior or segmentation faults upon
reload
B. It safely serializes the virtual functions along with data fields
C. The compiler issues a static_assert failure because the class is not trivially copyable
D. The stream automatically excludes the vptr pointer during the write sequence
Correct Answer: It writes the raw internal vptr address to disk, causing critical undefined behavior or segmentation faults
upon reload
Rationale: Using raw byte-wise write operations on polymorphic objects copies the internal virtual table pointer (vptr).
When reloaded into a different memory space or session, this stale address points to invalid memory, corrupting the
execution context. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 37 of 251

--- PAGE 38 ---
Q73. What happens if you open a file using ios::out | ios::app combined with an explicit seekp() invocation
to position 730?
ofstream out("log_{i}.txt", ios::out | ios::app);
out.seekp(0, ios::beg);
out << "Data";
A. The output data is unconditionally appended to the end of the file, completely ignoring seekp() instructions
B. The data is overwritten at the beginning of the file due to seekp() override
C. The stream enters an internal fail state and rejects the output operation
D. The file contents are cleared before writing
Correct Answer: The output data is unconditionally appended to the end of the file, completely ignoring seekp()
instructions
Rationale: When a file stream is opened in append mode (ios::app), all output operations are forced to the current end-of-
file before every write, effectively rendering any seekp() relocations inert. 
Q74. What is the exact functional distinction between using the method close() explicitly vs relying on the
stream class destructor instance 74?
{
    ofstream logFile("trace_{i}.txt");
    logFile << "Payload";
    // Line X: explicit close or let it go out of scope
}
A. Both flush buffers and release handles, but calling close() allows early resource reuse and explicit error
verification via fail()
B. The destructor does not automatically flush data buffers, causing potential silent data loss
C. Calling close() manually causes a double-free runtime panic when the destructor runs out of scope
D. The destructor can propagate file write errors through exception chains, whereas close() cannot
Correct Answer: Both flush buffers and release handles, but calling close() allows early resource reuse and explicit error
verification via fail()
Rationale: The destructor automatically closes the file handle and flushes remaining buffers, but it cannot safely return
errors or allow the program to verify successful closure before exiting the block scope. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 38 of 251

--- PAGE 39 ---
Q75. What is the behavior of the following C++ code snippet when opening a non-existent file for reading
with ifstream?
#include 
#include 
using namespace std;
int main() {
    ifstream file("ghost_file_{i}.txt");
    if (file) cout << "Success";
    else cout << "Failure";
    return 0;
}
A. Prints 'Failure' safely
B. Throws a runtime_error exception
C. Prints 'Success' with undefined behavior
D. Causes a compile-time syntax error
Correct Answer: Prints 'Failure' safely
Rationale: By default, ifstream does not throw exceptions upon failure to open a file unless explicit exception masks are
set via file.exceptions(). Instead, the stream state bits are set, and evaluating the stream object as a boolean returns false. 
Exception Handling
Q1. What critical condition arises if a class destructor throws an unhandled exception during stack
unwinding initiated by another active exception?
struct Guard_{i} {{
    ~Guard_{i}() {{ throw runtime_error("Nested fail"); }}
};
// Inside a function that throws a primary exception...
A. The application instantly halts with an unconditional call to std::terminate()
B. The original exception is overridden and replaced by the nested destructor exception
C. The runtime aggregates both exceptions into a compound linked exception list
D. The system ignores the destructor exception and resumes processing the primary catch block
Correct Answer: The application instantly halts with an unconditional call to std::terminate()
Rationale: C++ cannot process two concurrent active exceptions. If a destructor invoked during stack unwinding lets an
exception escape, the execution environment calls std::terminate() immediately. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 39 of 251

--- PAGE 40 ---
Q2. Analyze this constructor exception resource leak scenario. If an exception is thrown on line 2, which
destructors are invoked?
class ResourceA { public: ~ResourceA() { cout << "A destroyed"; } };
class Worker {
    ResourceA* res;
public:
    Worker() {
        res = new ResourceA();
        throw runtime_error("Crash");
    }
    ~Worker() { delete res; }
};
A. Neither Worker nor the heap-allocated ResourceA are cleaned up automatically, causing a raw memory leak
B. The Worker destructor is fully executed to clean up state
C. The pointer res is zeroed out by the compiler stack mechanism
D. The system forces a total clean up of all nested allocation blocks within the activation frame
Correct Answer: Neither Worker nor the heap-allocated ResourceA are cleaned up automatically, causing a raw memory
leak
Rationale: An object whose constructor throws an exception is deemed never fully constructed. Consequently, its
destructor is bypassed entirely, leaving raw heap allocations dangling unless managed by safe RAII wrappers. 
Q3. What is the semantic distinction between writing 'throw;' vs 'throw e;' within a nested catch block
matching reference variable 'e'?
catch (Base& e) {
    // Case A: throw;
    // Case B: throw e;
}
A. 'throw;' rethrows the original derived polymorphic object accurately, while 'throw e;' copies and slices it into a
Base object instance
B. 'throw;' resets the exception stack state completely, wiping performance benchmarks
C. 'throw e;' preserves runtime efficiency by passing the underlying tracking reference directly
D. They are identical in compilation structure and behavior
Correct Answer: 'throw;' rethrows the original derived polymorphic object accurately, while 'throw e;' copies and slices it
into a Base object instance
Rationale: The bare expression 'throw;' rethrows the original exception object preserving its dynamic runtime
characteristics. Re-throwing the local parameter via 'throw e;' induces slicing if 'e' is a reference to a base class wrapper. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 40 of 251

--- PAGE 41 ---
Q4. If a function is explicitly declared with the 'noexcept' specifier, but invokes a routine that throws an
exception at runtime, what occurs?
void critical_routine() noexcept {
    throw runtime_error("Bypassed safety");
}
A. The application terminates immediately via std::terminate() without completing remaining stack unwinding for
the block
B. The noexcept specifier is silently stripped and disregarded by the dynamic runtime stack
C. The exception is converted to an old-style std::unexpected signal channel
D. A basic runtime compiler alert is outputted, but normal operations continue
Correct Answer: The application terminates immediately via std::terminate() without completing remaining stack unwinding
for the block
Rationale: Violating a noexcept guarantee halts execution by jumping to std::terminate() directly. This bypasses the typical
stack unwinding flow, preventing execution of destructors outside the local scope. 
Q5. Examine the polymorphic exception slicing trap below. What will the following catch block hierarchy
output when BaseException is a public parent of DerivedException?
class Base {};
class Derived : public Base {};
// ... 
try {
    throw Derived();
} catch (Base b) {
    cout << "Caught Base";
} catch (Derived &d) {
    cout << "Caught Derived";
}
A. Prints 'Caught Base' due to object slicing by value catch blocks
B. Prints 'Caught Derived' because the runtime matches the dynamic sub-type
C. Produces a terminal compile error due to a blocked unreachable catch block
D. Triggers an unhandled standard exception crash sequence
Correct Answer: Prints 'Caught Base' due to object slicing by value catch blocks
Rationale: Catching exceptions by value causes object slicing. The base class handler accepts the derived instance,
truncates its specialized fields/methods, and shadows any subsequent derived references. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 41 of 251

B. The noexcept specifier is silently stripped and disregarded by the dynamic runtime stack
C. The exception is converted to an old-style std::unexpected signal channel
D. A basic runtime compiler alert is outputted, but normal operations continue
Correct Answer: The application terminates immediately via std::terminate() without completing remaining stack unwinding
for the block
Rationale: Violating a noexcept guarantee halts execution by jumping to std::terminate() directly. This bypasses the typical
stack unwinding flow, preventing execution of destructors outside the local scope.

Q30. Examine the polymorphic exception slicing trap below. What will the following catch block hierarchy
output when BaseException is a public parent of DerivedException?
class Base {};
class Derived : public Base {};
// ... 
try {
    throw Derived();
} catch (Base b) {
    cout << "Caught Base";
} catch (Derived &d) {
    cout << "Caught Derived";
}
A. Prints 'Caught Base' due to object slicing by value catch blocks
B. Prints 'Caught Derived' because the runtime matches the dynamic sub-type
C. Produces a terminal compile error due to a blocked unreachable catch block
D. Triggers an unhandled standard exception crash sequence
Correct Answer: Prints 'Caught Base' due to object slicing by value catch blocks
Rationale: Catching exceptions by value causes object slicing. The base class handler accepts the derived instance,
truncates its specialized fields/methods, and shadows any subsequent derived references. 
Q31. What critical condition arises if a class destructor throws an unhandled exception during stack
unwinding initiated by another active exception?
struct Guard_{i} {{
    ~Guard_{i}() {{ throw runtime_error("Nested fail"); }}
};
// Inside a function that throws a primary exception...
A. The application instantly halts with an unconditional call to std::terminate()
B. The original exception is overridden and replaced by the nested destructor exception
C. The runtime aggregates both exceptions into a compound linked exception list
D. The system ignores the destructor exception and resumes processing the primary catch block
Correct Answer: The application instantly halts with an unconditional call to std::terminate()
Rationale: C++ cannot process two concurrent active exceptions. If a destructor invoked during stack unwinding lets an
exception escape, the execution environment calls std::terminate() immediately. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 54 of 251

--- PAGE 55 ---
Q32. Analyze this constructor exception resource leak scenario. If an exception is thrown on line 32, which
destructors are invoked?
class ResourceA { public: ~ResourceA() { cout << "A destroyed"; } };
class Worker {
    ResourceA* res;
public:
    Worker() {
        res = new ResourceA();
        throw runtime_error("Crash");
    }
    ~Worker() { delete res; }
};
A. Neither Worker nor the heap-allocated ResourceA are cleaned up automatically, causing a raw memory leak
B. The Worker destructor is fully executed to clean up state
C. The pointer res is zeroed out by the compiler stack mechanism
D. The system forces a total clean up of all nested allocation blocks within the activation frame
Correct Answer: Neither Worker nor the heap-allocated ResourceA are cleaned up automatically, causing a raw memory
leak
Rationale: An object whose constructor throws an exception is deemed never fully constructed. Consequently, its
destructor is bypassed entirely, leaving raw heap allocations dangling unless managed by safe RAII wrappers. 
Q33. What is the semantic distinction between writing 'throw;' vs 'throw e;' within a nested catch block
matching reference variable 'e'?
catch (Base& e) {
    // Case A: throw;
    // Case B: throw e;
}
A. 'throw;' rethrows the original derived polymorphic object accurately, while 'throw e;' copies and slices it into a
Base object instance
B. 'throw;' resets the exception stack state completely, wiping performance benchmarks
C. 'throw e;' preserves runtime efficiency by passing the underlying tracking reference directly
D. They are identical in compilation structure and behavior
Correct Answer: 'throw;' rethrows the original derived polymorphic object accurately, while 'throw e;' copies and slices it
into a Base object instance
Rationale: The bare expression 'throw;' rethrows the original exception object preserving its dynamic runtime
characteristics. Re-throwing the local parameter via 'throw e;' induces slicing if 'e' is a reference to a base class wrapper. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 55 of 251

C. The pointer res is zeroed out by the compiler stack mechanism
D. The system forces a total clean up of all nested allocation blocks within the activation frame
Correct Answer: Neither Worker nor the heap-allocated ResourceA are cleaned up automatically, causing a raw memory
leak
Rationale: An object whose constructor throws an exception is deemed never fully constructed. Consequently, its
destructor is bypassed entirely, leaving raw heap allocations dangling unless managed by safe RAII wrappers. 
Q63. What is the semantic distinction between writing 'throw;' vs 'throw e;' within a nested catch block
matching reference variable 'e'?
catch (Base& e) {
    // Case A: throw;
    // Case B: throw e;
}
A. 'throw;' rethrows the original derived polymorphic object accurately, while 'throw e;' copies and slices it into a
Base object instance
B. 'throw;' resets the exception stack state completely, wiping performance benchmarks
C. 'throw e;' preserves runtime efficiency by passing the underlying tracking reference directly
D. They are identical in compilation structure and behavior
Correct Answer: 'throw;' rethrows the original derived polymorphic object accurately, while 'throw e;' copies and slices it
into a Base object instance
Rationale: The bare expression 'throw;' rethrows the original exception object preserving its dynamic runtime
characteristics. Re-throwing the local parameter via 'throw e;' induces slicing if 'e' is a reference to a base class wrapper. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 70 of 251

--- PAGE 71 ---
Q64. If a function is explicitly declared with the 'noexcept' specifier, but invokes a routine that throws an
exception at runtime, what occurs?
void critical_routine() noexcept {
    throw runtime_error("Bypassed safety");
}
A. The application terminates immediately via std::terminate() without completing remaining stack unwinding for
the block
B. The noexcept specifier is silently stripped and disregarded by the dynamic runtime stack
C. The exception is converted to an old-style std::unexpected signal channel
D. A basic runtime compiler alert is outputted, but normal operations continue
Correct Answer: The application terminates immediately via std::terminate() without completing remaining stack unwinding
for the block
Rationale: Violating a noexcept guarantee halts execution by jumping to std::terminate() directly. This bypasses the typical
stack unwinding flow, preventing execution of destructors outside the local scope. 
Q65. Examine the polymorphic exception slicing trap below. What will the following catch block hierarchy
output when BaseException is a public parent of DerivedException?
class Base {};
class Derived : public Base {};
// ... 
try {
    throw Derived();
} catch (Base b) {
    cout << "Caught Base";
} catch (Derived &d) {
    cout << "Caught Derived";
}
A. Prints 'Caught Base' due to object slicing by value catch blocks
B. Prints 'Caught Derived' because the runtime matches the dynamic sub-type
C. Produces a terminal compile error due to a blocked unreachable catch block
D. Triggers an unhandled standard exception crash sequence
Correct Answer: Prints 'Caught Base' due to object slicing by value catch blocks
Rationale: Catching exceptions by value causes object slicing. The base class handler accepts the derived instance,
truncates its specialized fields/methods, and shadows any subsequent derived references. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 71 of 251

--- PAGE 72 ---
Q66. What critical condition arises if a class destructor throws an unhandled exception during stack
unwinding initiated by another active exception?
struct Guard_{i} {{
    ~Guard_{i}() {{ throw runtime_error("Nested fail"); }}
};
// Inside a function that throws a primary exception...
A. The application instantly halts with an unconditional call to std::terminate()
B. The original exception is overridden and replaced by the nested destructor exception
C. The runtime aggregates both exceptions into a compound linked exception list
D. The system ignores the destructor exception and resumes processing the primary catch block
Correct Answer: The application instantly halts with an unconditional call to std::terminate()
Rationale: C++ cannot process two concurrent active exceptions. If a destructor invoked during stack unwinding lets an
exception escape, the execution environment calls std::terminate() immediately. 
Q67. Analyze this constructor exception resource leak scenario. If an exception is thrown on line 67, which
destructors are invoked?
class ResourceA { public: ~ResourceA() { cout << "A destroyed"; } };
class Worker {
    ResourceA* res;
public:
    Worker() {
        res = new ResourceA();
        throw runtime_error("Crash");
    }
    ~Worker() { delete res; }
};
A. Neither Worker nor the heap-allocated ResourceA are cleaned up automatically, causing a raw memory leak
B. The Worker destructor is fully executed to clean up state
C. The pointer res is zeroed out by the compiler stack mechanism
D. The system forces a total clean up of all nested allocation blocks within the activation frame
Correct Answer: Neither Worker nor the heap-allocated ResourceA are cleaned up automatically, causing a raw memory
leak
Rationale: An object whose constructor throws an exception is deemed never fully constructed. Consequently, its
destructor is bypassed entirely, leaving raw heap allocations dangling unless managed by safe RAII wrappers.

Object-Oriented Programming (OOP)
Q1. What is the outcome of attempting to call a virtual function from within a class base constructor block?
class Base {
public:
    Base() { log(); }
    virtual void log() { cout << "BaseLog"; }
};
class Derived : public Base {
public:
    void log() override { cout << "DerivedLog"; }
};
Derived d;
A. Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
B. Prints 'DerivedLog' via dynamic dispatch mechanisms
C. Triggers a runtime segmentation error due to an uninitialized vtable structure
D. The compiler replaces the call with a pure-virtual tracking warning
Correct Answer: Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
Rationale: During execution of a base constructor, the derived class elements do not exist yet. The object's virtual table
pointer (vptr) points to the base class vtable, resolving virtual calls to the base implementation. 
Q2. How does C++ resolve the classic Diamond Problem under multiple inheritance scenarios?
class A { public: int val; };
class B : public virtual A {};
class C : public virtual A {};
class D : public B, public C {};
A. Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class is
instantiated
B. The compiler aggregates distinct offsets and demands explicit scope resolution for every member access
C. The last class in the declaration order silently overwrites prior instances
D. Multiple inheritance of non-abstract classes is forbidden by modern C++ core specifications
Correct Answer: Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class
is instantiated
Rationale: Virtual inheritance guarantees that the ultimate derived class contains exactly one shared instance of the virtual
base class subobject, resolving structural member ambiguities. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 77 of 251

--- PAGE 78 ---
Q3. Analyze the following copy constructor and assignment operator interaction trap. What happens here?
class Sample {
public:
    Sample() {}
    Sample(const Sample& s) { cout << "Copy "; }
    Sample& operator=(const Sample& s) { cout << "Assign "; return *this; }
};
Sample s1;
Sample s2 = s1;
A. Prints 'Copy ' only
B. Prints 'Assign ' only
C. Prints 'Copy Assign ' sequentially
D. Triggers a compile failure due to ambiguous assignment syntax initialization
Correct Answer: Prints 'Copy ' only
Rationale: The line 'Sample s2 = s1;' is a declaration with initialization, invoking the copy constructor. The assignment
operator (`operator=`) is only called when an already-existing object is assigned a new value. 
Q4. What is the function of an explicit constructor marker in C++ class definitions?
class Box {
public:
    explicit Box(int size) {}
};
void process(Box b) {}
// ... process({i});
A. It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
B. It forces the object to be allocated exclusively on the system heap frame
C. It signals the compiler to inline all member functions of this class instance
D. It enforces const invariants across all internal member variables
Correct Answer: It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
Rationale: Declaring a single-parameter constructor 'explicit' prevents the compiler from performing silent, implicit
conversions from the parameter type to the class type during assignment or function calls. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 78 of 251

--- PAGE 79 ---
Q5. What occurs if a class defines a pure virtual function but does not override it in a child class, and you
attempt to instantiate that child class?
class Abstract { public: virtual void action() = 0; };
class Child : public Abstract {};
// ... Child c;
A. A compile-time error occurs because Child is also considered an abstract class and cannot be instantiated
B. The application compiles but crashes with a pure virtual call error if action is invoked
C. The compiler automatically injects a default empty implementation for action()
D. The program compiles but completely ignores any object initialization blocks
Correct Answer: A compile-time error occurs because Child is also considered an abstract class and cannot be
instantiated
Rationale: A class that inherits pure virtual functions remains abstract unless it overrides every pure virtual method with a
concrete implementation. Abstract classes cannot be instantiated. 
Q6. Evaluate the virtual destructor trap. If a base class pointer pointing to a derived object is deleted, and
the base class lacks a virtual destructor, what happens?
class Base { public: ~Base() { cout << "Base Subtype"; } };
class Derived : public Base { public: ~Derived() { cout << "Derived Subtype"; } };
Base* ptr = new Derived();
delete ptr;
A. Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
B. The runtime tracks allocations internally and calls both destructors safely
C. The compiler blocks the delete instruction with an explicit compile error
D. A runtime memory protection exception terminates the current process frame
Correct Answer: Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
Rationale: Deleting a derived object via a pointer to a base class lacking a virtual destructor causes undefined behavior. In
practice, only the base destructor is invoked, creating resource leaks.

Correct Answer: It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
Rationale: Declaring a single-parameter constructor 'explicit' prevents the compiler from performing silent, implicit
conversions from the parameter type to the class type during assignment or function calls.

Q23. What occurs if a class defines a pure virtual function but does not override it in a child class, and you
attempt to instantiate that child class?
class Abstract { public: virtual void action() = 0; };
class Child : public Abstract {};
// ... Child c;
A. A compile-time error occurs because Child is also considered an abstract class and cannot be instantiated
B. The application compiles but crashes with a pure virtual call error if action is invoked
C. The compiler automatically injects a default empty implementation for action()
D. The program compiles but completely ignores any object initialization blocks
Correct Answer: A compile-time error occurs because Child is also considered an abstract class and cannot be
instantiated
Rationale: A class that inherits pure virtual functions remains abstract unless it overrides every pure virtual method with a
concrete implementation. Abstract classes cannot be instantiated. 
Q24. Evaluate the virtual destructor trap. If a base class pointer pointing to a derived object is deleted, and
the base class lacks a virtual destructor, what happens?
class Base { public: ~Base() { cout << "Base Subtype"; } };
class Derived : public Base { public: ~Derived() { cout << "Derived Subtype"; } };
Base* ptr = new Derived();
delete ptr;
A. Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
B. The runtime tracks allocations internally and calls both destructors safely
C. The compiler blocks the delete instruction with an explicit compile error
D. A runtime memory protection exception terminates the current process frame
Correct Answer: Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
Rationale: Deleting a derived object via a pointer to a base class lacking a virtual destructor causes undefined behavior. In
practice, only the base destructor is invoked, creating resource leaks. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 88 of 251

--- PAGE 89 ---
Q25. What is the outcome of attempting to call a virtual function from within a class base constructor
block?
class Base {
public:
    Base() { log(); }
    virtual void log() { cout << "BaseLog"; }
};
class Derived : public Base {
public:
    void log() override { cout << "DerivedLog"; }
};
Derived d;
A. Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
B. Prints 'DerivedLog' via dynamic dispatch mechanisms
C. Triggers a runtime segmentation error due to an uninitialized vtable structure
D. The compiler replaces the call with a pure-virtual tracking warning
Correct Answer: Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
Rationale: During execution of a base constructor, the derived class elements do not exist yet. The object's virtual table
pointer (vptr) points to the base class vtable, resolving virtual calls to the base implementation. 
Q26. How does C++ resolve the classic Diamond Problem under multiple inheritance scenarios?
class A { public: int val; };
class B : public virtual A {};
class C : public virtual A {};
class D : public B, public C {};
A. Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class is
instantiated
B. The compiler aggregates distinct offsets and demands explicit scope resolution for every member access
C. The last class in the declaration order silently overwrites prior instances
D. Multiple inheritance of non-abstract classes is forbidden by modern C++ core specifications
Correct Answer: Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class
is instantiated
Rationale: Virtual inheritance guarantees that the ultimate derived class contains exactly one shared instance of the virtual
base class subobject, resolving structural member ambiguities. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 89 of 251

--- PAGE 90 ---
Q27. Analyze the following copy constructor and assignment operator interaction trap. What happens
here?
class Sample {
public:
    Sample() {}
    Sample(const Sample& s) { cout << "Copy "; }
    Sample& operator=(const Sample& s) { cout << "Assign "; return *this; }
};
Sample s1;
Sample s2 = s1;
A. Prints 'Copy ' only
B. Prints 'Assign ' only
C. Prints 'Copy Assign ' sequentially
D. Triggers a compile failure due to ambiguous assignment syntax initialization
Correct Answer: Prints 'Copy ' only
Rationale: The line 'Sample s2 = s1;' is a declaration with initialization, invoking the copy constructor. The assignment
operator (`operator=`) is only called when an already-existing object is assigned a new value.

attempt to instantiate that child class?
class Abstract { public: virtual void action() = 0; };
class Child : public Abstract {};
// ... Child c;
A. A compile-time error occurs because Child is also considered an abstract class and cannot be instantiated
B. The application compiles but crashes with a pure virtual call error if action is invoked
C. The compiler automatically injects a default empty implementation for action()
D. The program compiles but completely ignores any object initialization blocks
Correct Answer: A compile-time error occurs because Child is also considered an abstract class and cannot be
instantiated
Rationale: A class that inherits pure virtual functions remains abstract unless it overrides every pure virtual method with a
concrete implementation. Abstract classes cannot be instantiated. 
Q60. Evaluate the virtual destructor trap. If a base class pointer pointing to a derived object is deleted, and
the base class lacks a virtual destructor, what happens?
class Base { public: ~Base() { cout << "Base Subtype"; } };
class Derived : public Base { public: ~Derived() { cout << "Derived Subtype"; } };
Base* ptr = new Derived();
delete ptr;
A. Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
B. The runtime tracks allocations internally and calls both destructors safely
C. The compiler blocks the delete instruction with an explicit compile error
D. A runtime memory protection exception terminates the current process frame
Correct Answer: Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
Rationale: Deleting a derived object via a pointer to a base class lacking a virtual destructor causes undefined behavior. In
practice, only the base destructor is invoked, creating resource leaks. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 106 of 251

--- PAGE 107 ---
Q61. What is the outcome of attempting to call a virtual function from within a class base constructor
block?
class Base {
public:
    Base() { log(); }
    virtual void log() { cout << "BaseLog"; }
};
class Derived : public Base {
public:
    void log() override { cout << "DerivedLog"; }
};
Derived d;
A. Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
B. Prints 'DerivedLog' via dynamic dispatch mechanisms
C. Triggers a runtime segmentation error due to an uninitialized vtable structure
D. The compiler replaces the call with a pure-virtual tracking warning
Correct Answer: Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
Rationale: During execution of a base constructor, the derived class elements do not exist yet. The object's virtual table
pointer (vptr) points to the base class vtable, resolving virtual calls to the base implementation. 
Q62. How does C++ resolve the classic Diamond Problem under multiple inheritance scenarios?
class A { public: int val; };
class B : public virtual A {};
class C : public virtual A {};
class D : public B, public C {};
A. Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class is
instantiated
B. The compiler aggregates distinct offsets and demands explicit scope resolution for every member access
C. The last class in the declaration order silently overwrites prior instances
D. Multiple inheritance of non-abstract classes is forbidden by modern C++ core specifications
Correct Answer: Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class
is instantiated
Rationale: Virtual inheritance guarantees that the ultimate derived class contains exactly one shared instance of the virtual
base class subobject, resolving structural member ambiguities. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 107 of 251

--- PAGE 108 ---
Q63. Analyze the following copy constructor and assignment operator interaction trap. What happens
here?
class Sample {
public:
    Sample() {}
    Sample(const Sample& s) { cout << "Copy "; }
    Sample& operator=(const Sample& s) { cout << "Assign "; return *this; }
};
Sample s1;
Sample s2 = s1;
A. Prints 'Copy ' only
B. Prints 'Assign ' only
C. Prints 'Copy Assign ' sequentially
D. Triggers a compile failure due to ambiguous assignment syntax initialization
Correct Answer: Prints 'Copy ' only
Rationale: The line 'Sample s2 = s1;' is a declaration with initialization, invoking the copy constructor. The assignment
operator (`operator=`) is only called when an already-existing object is assigned a new value. 
Q64. What is the function of an explicit constructor marker in C++ class definitions?
class Box {
public:
    explicit Box(int size) {}
};
void process(Box b) {}
// ... process({i});
A. It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
B. It forces the object to be allocated exclusively on the system heap frame
C. It signals the compiler to inline all member functions of this class instance
D. It enforces const invariants across all internal member variables
Correct Answer: It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
Rationale: Declaring a single-parameter constructor 'explicit' prevents the compiler from performing silent, implicit
conversions from the parameter type to the class type during assignment or function calls.

D. The program compiles but completely ignores any object initialization blocks
Correct Answer: A compile-time error occurs because Child is also considered an abstract class and cannot be
instantiated
Rationale: A class that inherits pure virtual functions remains abstract unless it overrides every pure virtual method with a
concrete implementation. Abstract classes cannot be instantiated. 
Q96. Evaluate the virtual destructor trap. If a base class pointer pointing to a derived object is deleted, and
the base class lacks a virtual destructor, what happens?
class Base { public: ~Base() { cout << "Base Subtype"; } };
class Derived : public Base { public: ~Derived() { cout << "Derived Subtype"; } };
Base* ptr = new Derived();
delete ptr;
A. Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
B. The runtime tracks allocations internally and calls both destructors safely
C. The compiler blocks the delete instruction with an explicit compile error
D. A runtime memory protection exception terminates the current process frame
Correct Answer: Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
Rationale: Deleting a derived object via a pointer to a base class lacking a virtual destructor causes undefined behavior. In
practice, only the base destructor is invoked, creating resource leaks. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 124 of 251

--- PAGE 125 ---
Q97. What is the outcome of attempting to call a virtual function from within a class base constructor
block?
class Base {
public:
    Base() { log(); }
    virtual void log() { cout << "BaseLog"; }
};
class Derived : public Base {
public:
    void log() override { cout << "DerivedLog"; }
};
Derived d;
A. Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
B. Prints 'DerivedLog' via dynamic dispatch mechanisms
C. Triggers a runtime segmentation error due to an uninitialized vtable structure
D. The compiler replaces the call with a pure-virtual tracking warning
Correct Answer: Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
Rationale: During execution of a base constructor, the derived class elements do not exist yet. The object's virtual table
pointer (vptr) points to the base class vtable, resolving virtual calls to the base implementation. 
Q98. How does C++ resolve the classic Diamond Problem under multiple inheritance scenarios?
class A { public: int val; };
class B : public virtual A {};
class C : public virtual A {};
class D : public B, public C {};
A. Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class is
instantiated
B. The compiler aggregates distinct offsets and demands explicit scope resolution for every member access
C. The last class in the declaration order silently overwrites prior instances
D. Multiple inheritance of non-abstract classes is forbidden by modern C++ core specifications
Correct Answer: Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class
is instantiated
Rationale: Virtual inheritance guarantees that the ultimate derived class contains exactly one shared instance of the virtual
base class subobject, resolving structural member ambiguities. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 125 of 251

--- PAGE 126 ---
Q99. Analyze the following copy constructor and assignment operator interaction trap. What happens
here?
class Sample {
public:
    Sample() {}
    Sample(const Sample& s) { cout << "Copy "; }
    Sample& operator=(const Sample& s) { cout << "Assign "; return *this; }
};
Sample s1;
Sample s2 = s1;
A. Prints 'Copy ' only
B. Prints 'Assign ' only
C. Prints 'Copy Assign ' sequentially
D. Triggers a compile failure due to ambiguous assignment syntax initialization
Correct Answer: Prints 'Copy ' only
Rationale: The line 'Sample s2 = s1;' is a declaration with initialization, invoking the copy constructor. The assignment
operator (`operator=`) is only called when an already-existing object is assigned a new value. 
Q100. What is the function of an explicit constructor marker in C++ class definitions?
class Box {
public:
    explicit Box(int size) {}
};
void process(Box b) {}
// ... process({i});
A. It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
B. It forces the object to be allocated exclusively on the system heap frame
C. It signals the compiler to inline all member functions of this class instance
D. It enforces const invariants across all internal member variables
Correct Answer: It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
Rationale: Declaring a single-parameter constructor 'explicit' prevents the compiler from performing silent, implicit
conversions from the parameter type to the class type during assignment or function calls.

and the base class lacks a virtual destructor, what happens?
class Base { public: ~Base() { cout << "Base Subtype"; } };
class Derived : public Base { public: ~Derived() { cout << "Derived Subtype"; } };
Base* ptr = new Derived();
delete ptr;
A. Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
B. The runtime tracks allocations internally and calls both destructors safely
C. The compiler blocks the delete instruction with an explicit compile error
D. A runtime memory protection exception terminates the current process frame
Correct Answer: Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
Rationale: Deleting a derived object via a pointer to a base class lacking a virtual destructor causes undefined behavior. In
practice, only the base destructor is invoked, creating resource leaks. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 142 of 251

--- PAGE 143 ---
Q133. What is the outcome of attempting to call a virtual function from within a class base constructor
block?
class Base {
public:
    Base() { log(); }
    virtual void log() { cout << "BaseLog"; }
};
class Derived : public Base {
public:
    void log() override { cout << "DerivedLog"; }
};
Derived d;
A. Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
B. Prints 'DerivedLog' via dynamic dispatch mechanisms
C. Triggers a runtime segmentation error due to an uninitialized vtable structure
D. The compiler replaces the call with a pure-virtual tracking warning
Correct Answer: Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
Rationale: During execution of a base constructor, the derived class elements do not exist yet. The object's virtual table
pointer (vptr) points to the base class vtable, resolving virtual calls to the base implementation. 
Q134. How does C++ resolve the classic Diamond Problem under multiple inheritance scenarios?
class A { public: int val; };
class B : public virtual A {};
class C : public virtual A {};
class D : public B, public C {};
A. Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class is
instantiated
B. The compiler aggregates distinct offsets and demands explicit scope resolution for every member access
C. The last class in the declaration order silently overwrites prior instances
D. Multiple inheritance of non-abstract classes is forbidden by modern C++ core specifications
Correct Answer: Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class
is instantiated
Rationale: Virtual inheritance guarantees that the ultimate derived class contains exactly one shared instance of the virtual
base class subobject, resolving structural member ambiguities. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 143 of 251

--- PAGE 144 ---
Q135. Analyze the following copy constructor and assignment operator interaction trap. What happens
here?
class Sample {
public:
    Sample() {}
    Sample(const Sample& s) { cout << "Copy "; }
    Sample& operator=(const Sample& s) { cout << "Assign "; return *this; }
};
Sample s1;
Sample s2 = s1;
A. Prints 'Copy ' only
B. Prints 'Assign ' only
C. Prints 'Copy Assign ' sequentially
D. Triggers a compile failure due to ambiguous assignment syntax initialization
Correct Answer: Prints 'Copy ' only
Rationale: The line 'Sample s2 = s1;' is a declaration with initialization, invoking the copy constructor. The assignment
operator (`operator=`) is only called when an already-existing object is assigned a new value. 
Q136. What is the function of an explicit constructor marker in C++ class definitions?
class Box {
public:
    explicit Box(int size) {}
};
void process(Box b) {}
// ... process({i});
A. It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
B. It forces the object to be allocated exclusively on the system heap frame
C. It signals the compiler to inline all member functions of this class instance
D. It enforces const invariants across all internal member variables
Correct Answer: It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
Rationale: Declaring a single-parameter constructor 'explicit' prevents the compiler from performing silent, implicit
conversions from the parameter type to the class type during assignment or function calls.

B. The runtime tracks allocations internally and calls both destructors safely
C. The compiler blocks the delete instruction with an explicit compile error
D. A runtime memory protection exception terminates the current process frame
Correct Answer: Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
Rationale: Deleting a derived object via a pointer to a base class lacking a virtual destructor causes undefined behavior. In
practice, only the base destructor is invoked, creating resource leaks. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 160 of 251

--- PAGE 161 ---
Q169. What is the outcome of attempting to call a virtual function from within a class base constructor
block?
class Base {
public:
    Base() { log(); }
    virtual void log() { cout << "BaseLog"; }
};
class Derived : public Base {
public:
    void log() override { cout << "DerivedLog"; }
};
Derived d;
A. Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
B. Prints 'DerivedLog' via dynamic dispatch mechanisms
C. Triggers a runtime segmentation error due to an uninitialized vtable structure
D. The compiler replaces the call with a pure-virtual tracking warning
Correct Answer: Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
Rationale: During execution of a base constructor, the derived class elements do not exist yet. The object's virtual table
pointer (vptr) points to the base class vtable, resolving virtual calls to the base implementation. 
Q170. How does C++ resolve the classic Diamond Problem under multiple inheritance scenarios?
class A { public: int val; };
class B : public virtual A {};
class C : public virtual A {};
class D : public B, public C {};
A. Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class is
instantiated
B. The compiler aggregates distinct offsets and demands explicit scope resolution for every member access
C. The last class in the declaration order silently overwrites prior instances
D. Multiple inheritance of non-abstract classes is forbidden by modern C++ core specifications
Correct Answer: Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class
is instantiated
Rationale: Virtual inheritance guarantees that the ultimate derived class contains exactly one shared instance of the virtual
base class subobject, resolving structural member ambiguities. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 161 of 251

Q171. Analyze the following copy constructor and assignment operator interaction trap. What happens
here?
class Sample {
public:
    Sample() {}
    Sample(const Sample& s) { cout << "Copy "; }
    Sample& operator=(const Sample& s) { cout << "Assign "; return *this; }
};
Sample s1;
Sample s2 = s1;
A. Prints 'Copy ' only
B. Prints 'Assign ' only
C. Prints 'Copy Assign ' sequentially
D. Triggers a compile failure due to ambiguous assignment syntax initialization
Correct Answer: Prints 'Copy ' only
Rationale: The line 'Sample s2 = s1;' is a declaration with initialization, invoking the copy constructor. The assignment
operator (`operator=`) is only called when an already-existing object is assigned a new value. 
Q172. What is the function of an explicit constructor marker in C++ class definitions?
class Box {
public:
    explicit Box(int size) {}
};
void process(Box b) {}
// ... process({i});
A. It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
B. It forces the object to be allocated exclusively on the system heap frame
C. It signals the compiler to inline all member functions of this class instance
D. It enforces const invariants across all internal member variables
Correct Answer: It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
Rationale: Declaring a single-parameter constructor 'explicit' prevents the compiler from performing silent, implicit
conversions from the parameter type to the class type during assignment or function calls.

C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 178 of 251

--- PAGE 179 ---
Q205. What is the outcome of attempting to call a virtual function from within a class base constructor
block?
class Base {
public:
    Base() { log(); }
    virtual void log() { cout << "BaseLog"; }
};
class Derived : public Base {
public:
    void log() override { cout << "DerivedLog"; }
};
Derived d;
A. Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
B. Prints 'DerivedLog' via dynamic dispatch mechanisms
C. Triggers a runtime segmentation error due to an uninitialized vtable structure
D. The compiler replaces the call with a pure-virtual tracking warning
Correct Answer: Prints 'BaseLog' because the derived part of the object is not yet constructed
Rationale: During execution of a base constructor, the derived class elements do not exist yet. The object's virtual table
pointer (vptr) points to the base class vtable, resolving virtual calls to the base implementation. 
Q206. How does C++ resolve the classic Diamond Problem under multiple inheritance scenarios?
class A { public: int val; };
class B : public virtual A {};
class C : public virtual A {};
class D : public B, public C {};
A. Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class is
instantiated
B. The compiler aggregates distinct offsets and demands explicit scope resolution for every member access
C. The last class in the declaration order silently overwrites prior instances
D. Multiple inheritance of non-abstract classes is forbidden by modern C++ core specifications
Correct Answer: Using the 'virtual' keyword during inheritance ensures only a single shared instance of the top base class
is instantiated
Rationale: Virtual inheritance guarantees that the ultimate derived class contains exactly one shared instance of the virtual
base class subobject, resolving structural member ambiguities. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 179 of 251

--- PAGE 180 ---
Q207. Analyze the following copy constructor and assignment operator interaction trap. What happens
here?
class Sample {
public:
    Sample() {}
    Sample(const Sample& s) { cout << "Copy "; }
    Sample& operator=(const Sample& s) { cout << "Assign "; return *this; }
};
Sample s1;
Sample s2 = s1;
A. Prints 'Copy ' only
B. Prints 'Assign ' only
C. Prints 'Copy Assign ' sequentially
D. Triggers a compile failure due to ambiguous assignment syntax initialization
Correct Answer: Prints 'Copy ' only
Rationale: The line 'Sample s2 = s1;' is a declaration with initialization, invoking the copy constructor. The assignment
operator (`operator=`) is only called when an already-existing object is assigned a new value. 
Q208. What is the function of an explicit constructor marker in C++ class definitions?
class Box {
public:
    explicit Box(int size) {}
};
void process(Box b) {}
// ... process({i});
A. It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
B. It forces the object to be allocated exclusively on the system heap frame
C. It signals the compiler to inline all member functions of this class instance
D. It enforces const invariants across all internal member variables
Correct Answer: It prevents implicit conversion operations, forcing the user to call the constructor explicitly via type syntax
Rationale: Declaring a single-parameter constructor 'explicit' prevents the compiler from performing silent, implicit
conversions from the parameter type to the class type during assignment or function calls. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 180 of 251

--- PAGE 181 ---
Q209. What occurs if a class defines a pure virtual function but does not override it in a child class, and
you attempt to instantiate that child class?
class Abstract { public: virtual void action() = 0; };
class Child : public Abstract {};
// ... Child c;
A. A compile-time error occurs because Child is also considered an abstract class and cannot be instantiated
B. The application compiles but crashes with a pure virtual call error if action is invoked
C. The compiler automatically injects a default empty implementation for action()
D. The program compiles but completely ignores any object initialization blocks
Correct Answer: A compile-time error occurs because Child is also considered an abstract class and cannot be
instantiated
Rationale: A class that inherits pure virtual functions remains abstract unless it overrides every pure virtual method with a
concrete implementation. Abstract classes cannot be instantiated. 
Q210. Evaluate the virtual destructor trap. If a base class pointer pointing to a derived object is deleted,
and the base class lacks a virtual destructor, what happens?
class Base { public: ~Base() { cout << "Base Subtype"; } };
class Derived : public Base { public: ~Derived() { cout << "Derived Subtype"; } };
Base* ptr = new Derived();
delete ptr;
A. Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
B. The runtime tracks allocations internally and calls both destructors safely
C. The compiler blocks the delete instruction with an explicit compile error
D. A runtime memory protection exception terminates the current process frame
Correct Answer: Only the Base destructor runs, leaving the Derived resources leaking and causing undefined behavior
Rationale: Deleting a derived object via a pointer to a base class lacking a virtual destructor causes undefined behavior. In
practice, only the base destructor is invoked, creating resource leaks.

Functions and Templates
Q1. What error occurs if a template function definition is placed entirely within a separate implementation
file (.cpp) and invoked from another module?
// math_templates.cpp: template T add(T a, T b) { return a+b; }
// main.cpp: external link invocation...
A. A linker error occurs because templates must be visible during compilation for instantiation
B. A syntax error is flagged by the compiler parser inside main.cpp
C. The application experiences a runtime crash when calling the template
D. The compiler builds a slow fallback variant using void pointers
Correct Answer: A linker error occurs because templates must be visible during compilation for instantiation
Rationale: Templates are blueprints, not functions. The compiler must see the full template definition to instantiate code for
a specific type. Separation into a separate translation unit leads to unresolved external symbols at link time. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 184 of 251

--- PAGE 185 ---
Q2. What is the effect of applying the 'inline' keyword to a function definition in modern C++ optimization
environments?
inline int processData(int x) { return x * {i}; }
A. It serves as a compiler hint for inline expansion, but the compiler can freely ignore it based on optimization
strategies
B. It strictly forces inline replacement, bypassing all standard compiler safety checks
C. It restricts the function from being defined in header files across multiple units
D. It moves the execution context directly to the hardware kernel stack
Correct Answer: It serves as a compiler hint for inline expansion, but the compiler can freely ignore it based on
optimization strategies
Rationale: The 'inline' keyword is primarily a hint to the compiler regarding optimization. Modern compilers rely on their
own heuristics to decide whether to inline code, though 'inline' still permits defining functions in header files without violating
the One Definition Rule (ODR). 
Q3. Analyze template deduction with reference parameters. What type is deduced for T here?
template void inspect(T& param) {}
const int val = {i};
inspect(val);
A. T is deduced as 'const int'
B. T is deduced as 'int', and param becomes 'int&'
C. T is deduced as 'int', and param becomes 'const int&'
D. Compilation fails due to a const qualifier mismatch
Correct Answer: T is deduced as 'const int'
Rationale: When passing a const argument to a template function accepting a reference parameter (`T&`), the constness
is preserved, deducing `T` as `const int` so that `param` becomes `const int&`. 
Q4. What occurs during template class template argument deduction (CTAD) if no explicit arguments are
specified?
template struct Wrapper { Wrapper(T val) {} };
Wrapper w({i});
A. The compiler deduces the type automatically from the constructor arguments (C++17 and later)
B. A mandatory compile error occurs demanding explicit template parameter brackets
C. The type defaults to int or double depending on system size configuration
D. The object is treated as a dynamic wrapper instance type
Correct Answer: The compiler deduces the type automatically from the constructor arguments (C++17 and later)
Rationale: Class Template Argument Deduction (CTAD), introduced in C++17, allows the compiler to deduce template
arguments from the types of constructor arguments. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 185 of 251

--- PAGE 186 ---
Q5. How does the C++ compiler handle non-template function overloads relative to template functions
when an exact match is found for both?
template void print(T val) { cout << "Template"; }
void print(int val) { cout << "Non-Template"; }
// ... print({i});
A. The non-template function is preferred over the template function when they provide an identical parameter
match
B. The template instantiation always takes precedence due to compilation ordering
C. The compiler generates an ambiguity error, demanding explicit qualification brackets
D. The choice is resolved randomly depending on optimization levels
Correct Answer: The non-template function is preferred over the template function when they provide an identical
parameter match
Rationale: During overload resolution, if a non-template function match is identical to a template specialization, the non-
template version takes precedence.

template struct Wrapper { Wrapper(T val) {} };
Wrapper w({i});
A. The compiler deduces the type automatically from the constructor arguments (C++17 and later)
B. A mandatory compile error occurs demanding explicit template parameter brackets
C. The type defaults to int or double depending on system size configuration
D. The object is treated as a dynamic wrapper instance type
Correct Answer: The compiler deduces the type automatically from the constructor arguments (C++17 and later)
Rationale: Class Template Argument Deduction (CTAD), introduced in C++17, allows the compiler to deduce template
arguments from the types of constructor arguments. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 195 of 251

--- PAGE 196 ---
Q30. How does the C++ compiler handle non-template function overloads relative to template functions
when an exact match is found for both?
template void print(T val) { cout << "Template"; }
void print(int val) { cout << "Non-Template"; }
// ... print({i});
A. The non-template function is preferred over the template function when they provide an identical parameter
match
B. The template instantiation always takes precedence due to compilation ordering
C. The compiler generates an ambiguity error, demanding explicit qualification brackets
D. The choice is resolved randomly depending on optimization levels
Correct Answer: The non-template function is preferred over the template function when they provide an identical
parameter match
Rationale: During overload resolution, if a non-template function match is identical to a template specialization, the non-
template version takes precedence. 
Q31. What error occurs if a template function definition is placed entirely within a separate implementation
file (.cpp) and invoked from another module?
// math_templates.cpp: template T add(T a, T b) { return a+b; }
// main.cpp: external link invocation...
A. A linker error occurs because templates must be visible during compilation for instantiation
B. A syntax error is flagged by the compiler parser inside main.cpp
C. The application experiences a runtime crash when calling the template
D. The compiler builds a slow fallback variant using void pointers
Correct Answer: A linker error occurs because templates must be visible during compilation for instantiation
Rationale: Templates are blueprints, not functions. The compiler must see the full template definition to instantiate code for
a specific type. Separation into a separate translation unit leads to unresolved external symbols at link time. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 196 of 251

--- PAGE 197 ---
Q32. What is the effect of applying the 'inline' keyword to a function definition in modern C++ optimization
environments?
inline int processData(int x) { return x * {i}; }
A. It serves as a compiler hint for inline expansion, but the compiler can freely ignore it based on optimization
strategies
B. It strictly forces inline replacement, bypassing all standard compiler safety checks
C. It restricts the function from being defined in header files across multiple units
D. It moves the execution context directly to the hardware kernel stack
Correct Answer: It serves as a compiler hint for inline expansion, but the compiler can freely ignore it based on
optimization strategies
Rationale: The 'inline' keyword is primarily a hint to the compiler regarding optimization. Modern compilers rely on their
own heuristics to decide whether to inline code, though 'inline' still permits defining functions in header files without violating
the One Definition Rule (ODR). 
Q33. Analyze template deduction with reference parameters. What type is deduced for T here?
template void inspect(T& param) {}
const int val = {i};
inspect(val);
A. T is deduced as 'const int'
B. T is deduced as 'int', and param becomes 'int&'
C. T is deduced as 'int', and param becomes 'const int&'
D. Compilation fails due to a const qualifier mismatch
Correct Answer: T is deduced as 'const int'
Rationale: When passing a const argument to a template function accepting a reference parameter (`T&`), the constness
is preserved, deducing `T` as `const int` so that `param` becomes `const int&`.

Correct Answer: The compiler deduces the type automatically from the constructor arguments (C++17 and later)
Rationale: Class Template Argument Deduction (CTAD), introduced in C++17, allows the compiler to deduce template
arguments from the types of constructor arguments. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 211 of 251

--- PAGE 212 ---
Q70. How does the C++ compiler handle non-template function overloads relative to template functions
when an exact match is found for both?
template void print(T val) { cout << "Template"; }
void print(int val) { cout << "Non-Template"; }
// ... print({i});
A. The non-template function is preferred over the template function when they provide an identical parameter
match
B. The template instantiation always takes precedence due to compilation ordering
C. The compiler generates an ambiguity error, demanding explicit qualification brackets
D. The choice is resolved randomly depending on optimization levels
Correct Answer: The non-template function is preferred over the template function when they provide an identical
parameter match
Rationale: During overload resolution, if a non-template function match is identical to a template specialization, the non-
template version takes precedence. 
Q71. What error occurs if a template function definition is placed entirely within a separate implementation
file (.cpp) and invoked from another module?
// math_templates.cpp: template T add(T a, T b) { return a+b; }
// main.cpp: external link invocation...
A. A linker error occurs because templates must be visible during compilation for instantiation
B. A syntax error is flagged by the compiler parser inside main.cpp
C. The application experiences a runtime crash when calling the template
D. The compiler builds a slow fallback variant using void pointers
Correct Answer: A linker error occurs because templates must be visible during compilation for instantiation
Rationale: Templates are blueprints, not functions. The compiler must see the full template definition to instantiate code for
a specific type. Separation into a separate translation unit leads to unresolved external symbols at link time. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 212 of 251

--- PAGE 213 ---
Q72. What is the effect of applying the 'inline' keyword to a function definition in modern C++ optimization
environments?
inline int processData(int x) { return x * {i}; }
A. It serves as a compiler hint for inline expansion, but the compiler can freely ignore it based on optimization
strategies
B. It strictly forces inline replacement, bypassing all standard compiler safety checks
C. It restricts the function from being defined in header files across multiple units
D. It moves the execution context directly to the hardware kernel stack
Correct Answer: It serves as a compiler hint for inline expansion, but the compiler can freely ignore it based on
optimization strategies
Rationale: The 'inline' keyword is primarily a hint to the compiler regarding optimization. Modern compilers rely on their
own heuristics to decide whether to inline code, though 'inline' still permits defining functions in header files without violating
the One Definition Rule (ODR). 
Q73. Analyze template deduction with reference parameters. What type is deduced for T here?
template void inspect(T& param) {}
const int val = {i};
inspect(val);
A. T is deduced as 'const int'
B. T is deduced as 'int', and param becomes 'int&'
C. T is deduced as 'int', and param becomes 'const int&'
D. Compilation fails due to a const qualifier mismatch
Correct Answer: T is deduced as 'const int'
Rationale: When passing a const argument to a template function accepting a reference parameter (`T&`), the constness
is preserved, deducing `T` as `const int` so that `param` becomes `const int&`. 
Q74. What occurs during template class template argument deduction (CTAD) if no explicit arguments are
specified?
template struct Wrapper { Wrapper(T val) {} };
Wrapper w({i});
A. The compiler deduces the type automatically from the constructor arguments (C++17 and later)
B. A mandatory compile error occurs demanding explicit template parameter brackets
C. The type defaults to int or double depending on system size configuration
D. The object is treated as a dynamic wrapper instance type
Correct Answer: The compiler deduces the type automatically from the constructor arguments (C++17 and later)
Rationale: Class Template Argument Deduction (CTAD), introduced in C++17, allows the compiler to deduce template
arguments from the types of constructor arguments.
