Strings, Pointers, and STL Containers
Q1. What is the functional difference between accessing an out-of-bounds index via vector::operator[] vs
using vector::at()?
vector v(5);
// Case A: v[10] = 1;
// Case B: v.at(10) = 1;
A. operator[] triggers undefined behavior without bounds checking, while at() throws a std::out_of_range
exception
B. operator[] performs strict checks and terminates the program safely
C. at() is slower because it allocates memory dynamically on every invocation
D. operator[] converts the out-of-bounds offset into a valid wrap-around index
Correct Answer: operator[] triggers undefined behavior without bounds checking, while at() throws a std::out_of_range
exception
Rationale: `operator[]` prioritizes raw performance and omits bounds checking, leading to undefined behavior if the index
is out of bounds. The `at()` member function provides safety by performing explicit bounds checks and throwing
`std::out_of_range` on failure. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 214 of 251

--- PAGE 215 ---
Q2. How do lambda capture expressions change variable behavior when using `[=]` vs `[&]` inside code
block 2?
int factor = {i};
auto lamp = [=](int x) { return x * factor; };
A. `[=]` captures variables by value (read-only copy unless marked mutable), whereas `[&]` captures variables by
reference
B. `[=]` restricts access to global namespace structures only
C. `[&]` creates copies on the stack frame to secure execution states
D. They are optimized to behave identically by modern compiler links
Correct Answer: `[=]` captures variables by value (read-only copy unless marked mutable), whereas `[&]` captures
variables by reference
Rationale: The capture default `[=]` captures all used automatic variables by copy-value, preventing modification of the
original outer variables. The reference default `[&]` captures by reference, allowing direct modification. 
Q3. What is the time complexity of looking up an item inside a `std::unordered_map` vs a `std::map`
container?
// Lookup metrics for element {i}
A. Unordered_map offers O(1) average time complexity, while map offers O(log n) logarithmic time complexity
B. Both provide O(1) guaranteed lookup execution boundaries
C. Map is faster because it uses contiguous vector backings
D. Unordered_map operates on an O(n log n) red-black tree hierarchy
Correct Answer: Unordered_map offers O(1) average time complexity, while map offers O(log n) logarithmic time
complexity
Rationale: `std::unordered_map` relies on hash tables, offering an average lookup complexity of $O(1)$. `std::map` is
structured as a balanced binary search tree (typically a red-black tree), guaranteeing $O(\log n)$ lookup performance. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 215 of 251

--- PAGE 216 ---
Q4. What pointer state problem arises from using raw pointers alongside `std::string::c_str()` when the
parent string object undergoes modification or goes out of scope?
const char* ptr = nullptr;
{
    string s = "Data_{i}";
    ptr = s.c_str();
}
// Accessing ptr here...
A. ptr becomes a dangling pointer pointing to deallocated memory, causing undefined behavior
B. The compiler automatically pins the string memory buffer to avoid cleanup leaks
C. The character pointer remains valid indefinitely because string copies data to static storage
D. The pointer is automatically reset to a safe null address pointer
Correct Answer: ptr becomes a dangling pointer pointing to deallocated memory, causing undefined behavior
Rationale: The `c_str()` method returns a pointer to the string's internal character array. If the parent string is modified or
goes out of scope, that internal array may be reallocated or deallocated, leaving the pointer dangling. 
Q5. What is the hidden cost or behavioral risk of invoking vector::push_back() inside a loop that causes
frequent capacity reallocations?
vector vec;
for(int i=0; i<{i*100}; ++i) vec.push_back(i);
A. It forces reallocation, invalidating all outstanding iterators, references, and pointers to its elements
B. It triggers a standard bad_alloc crash sequence whenever capacity doubles
C. The elements are rearranged into non-contiguous heap spaces
D. It limits the capacity growth pattern to a strictly linear increment scale
Correct Answer: It forces reallocation, invalidating all outstanding iterators, references, and pointers to its elements
Rationale: When a vector exceeds its current capacity, it allocates a new, larger memory block, copies or moves the
existing elements to it, and releases the old storage. This invalidates all existing iterators, references, and pointers.

goes out of scope, that internal array may be reallocated or deallocated, leaving the pointer dangling.

Q30. What is the hidden cost or behavioral risk of invoking vector::push_back() inside a loop that causes
frequent capacity reallocations?
vector vec;
for(int i=0; i<{i*100}; ++i) vec.push_back(i);
A. It forces reallocation, invalidating all outstanding iterators, references, and pointers to its elements
B. It triggers a standard bad_alloc crash sequence whenever capacity doubles
C. The elements are rearranged into non-contiguous heap spaces
D. It limits the capacity growth pattern to a strictly linear increment scale
Correct Answer: It forces reallocation, invalidating all outstanding iterators, references, and pointers to its elements
Rationale: When a vector exceeds its current capacity, it allocates a new, larger memory block, copies or moves the
existing elements to it, and releases the old storage. This invalidates all existing iterators, references, and pointers. 
Q31. What is the functional difference between accessing an out-of-bounds index via vector::operator[] vs
using vector::at()?
vector v(5);
// Case A: v[10] = 1;
// Case B: v.at(10) = 1;
A. operator[] triggers undefined behavior without bounds checking, while at() throws a std::out_of_range
exception
B. operator[] performs strict checks and terminates the program safely
C. at() is slower because it allocates memory dynamically on every invocation
D. operator[] converts the out-of-bounds offset into a valid wrap-around index
Correct Answer: operator[] triggers undefined behavior without bounds checking, while at() throws a std::out_of_range
exception
Rationale: `operator[]` prioritizes raw performance and omits bounds checking, leading to undefined behavior if the index
is out of bounds. The `at()` member function provides safety by performing explicit bounds checks and throwing
`std::out_of_range` on failure. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 229 of 251

--- PAGE 230 ---
Q32. How do lambda capture expressions change variable behavior when using `[=]` vs `[&]` inside code
block 32?
int factor = {i};
auto lamp = [=](int x) { return x * factor; };
A. `[=]` captures variables by value (read-only copy unless marked mutable), whereas `[&]` captures variables by
reference
B. `[=]` restricts access to global namespace structures only
C. `[&]` creates copies on the stack frame to secure execution states
D. They are optimized to behave identically by modern compiler links
Correct Answer: `[=]` captures variables by value (read-only copy unless marked mutable), whereas `[&]` captures
variables by reference
Rationale: The capture default `[=]` captures all used automatic variables by copy-value, preventing modification of the
original outer variables. The reference default `[&]` captures by reference, allowing direct modification. 
Q33. What is the time complexity of looking up an item inside a `std::unordered_map` vs a `std::map`
container?
// Lookup metrics for element {i}
A. Unordered_map offers O(1) average time complexity, while map offers O(log n) logarithmic time complexity
B. Both provide O(1) guaranteed lookup execution boundaries
C. Map is faster because it uses contiguous vector backings
D. Unordered_map operates on an O(n log n) red-black tree hierarchy
Correct Answer: Unordered_map offers O(1) average time complexity, while map offers O(log n) logarithmic time
complexity
Rationale: `std::unordered_map` relies on hash tables, offering an average lookup complexity of $O(1)$. `std::map` is
structured as a balanced binary search tree (typically a red-black tree), guaranteeing $O(\log n)$ lookup performance.

Q64. What pointer state problem arises from using raw pointers alongside `std::string::c_str()` when the
parent string object undergoes modification or goes out of scope?
const char* ptr = nullptr;
{
    string s = "Data_{i}";
    ptr = s.c_str();
}
// Accessing ptr here...
A. ptr becomes a dangling pointer pointing to deallocated memory, causing undefined behavior
B. The compiler automatically pins the string memory buffer to avoid cleanup leaks
C. The character pointer remains valid indefinitely because string copies data to static storage
D. The pointer is automatically reset to a safe null address pointer
Correct Answer: ptr becomes a dangling pointer pointing to deallocated memory, causing undefined behavior
Rationale: The `c_str()` method returns a pointer to the string's internal character array. If the parent string is modified or
goes out of scope, that internal array may be reallocated or deallocated, leaving the pointer dangling. 
Q65. What is the hidden cost or behavioral risk of invoking vector::push_back() inside a loop that causes
frequent capacity reallocations?
vector vec;
for(int i=0; i<{i*100}; ++i) vec.push_back(i);
A. It forces reallocation, invalidating all outstanding iterators, references, and pointers to its elements
B. It triggers a standard bad_alloc crash sequence whenever capacity doubles
C. The elements are rearranged into non-contiguous heap spaces
D. It limits the capacity growth pattern to a strictly linear increment scale
Correct Answer: It forces reallocation, invalidating all outstanding iterators, references, and pointers to its elements
Rationale: When a vector exceeds its current capacity, it allocates a new, larger memory block, copies or moves the
existing elements to it, and releases the old storage. This invalidates all existing iterators, references, and pointers. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 246 of 251

--- PAGE 247 ---
Q66. What is the functional difference between accessing an out-of-bounds index via vector::operator[] vs
using vector::at()?
vector v(5);
// Case A: v[10] = 1;
// Case B: v.at(10) = 1;
A. operator[] triggers undefined behavior without bounds checking, while at() throws a std::out_of_range
exception
B. operator[] performs strict checks and terminates the program safely
C. at() is slower because it allocates memory dynamically on every invocation
D. operator[] converts the out-of-bounds offset into a valid wrap-around index
Correct Answer: operator[] triggers undefined behavior without bounds checking, while at() throws a std::out_of_range
exception
Rationale: `operator[]` prioritizes raw performance and omits bounds checking, leading to undefined behavior if the index
is out of bounds. The `at()` member function provides safety by performing explicit bounds checks and throwing
`std::out_of_range` on failure. 
Q67. How do lambda capture expressions change variable behavior when using `[=]` vs `[&]` inside code
block 67?
int factor = {i};
auto lamp = [=](int x) { return x * factor; };
A. `[=]` captures variables by value (read-only copy unless marked mutable), whereas `[&]` captures variables by
reference
B. `[=]` restricts access to global namespace structures only
C. `[&]` creates copies on the stack frame to secure execution states
D. They are optimized to behave identically by modern compiler links
Correct Answer: `[=]` captures variables by value (read-only copy unless marked mutable), whereas `[&]` captures
variables by reference
Rationale: The capture default `[=]` captures all used automatic variables by copy-value, preventing modification of the
original outer variables. The reference default `[&]` captures by reference, allowing direct modification. 
C++ Advanced Master Question Bank Page 247 of 251

--- PAGE 248 ---
Q68. What is the time complexity of looking up an item inside a `std::unordered_map` vs a `std::map`
container?
// Lookup metrics for element {i}
A. Unordered_map offers O(1) average time complexity, while map offers O(log n) logarithmic time complexity
B. Both provide O(1) guaranteed lookup execution boundaries
C. Map is faster because it uses contiguous vector backings
D. Unordered_map operates on an O(n log n) red-black tree hierarchy
Correct Answer: Unordered_map offers O(1) average time complexity, while map offers O(log n) logarithmic time
complexity
Rationale: `std::unordered_map` relies on hash tables, offering an average lookup complexity of $O(1)$. `std::map` is
structured as a balanced binary search tree (typically a red-black tree), guaranteeing $O(\log n)$ lookup performance.
